and Alagan Anpalagan Alagan Anpalagan SciProfiles Scilit Preprints.org Google Scholar 2  1 Wireless and Artificial Intelligence Laboratory (WAI Lab), Instituto Nacional de Telecomunicações, Santa Rita do Sapucaí 37536-001, Brazil 2 Department of Electrical, Computer and Biomedical Engineering, Toronto Metropolitan University, Toronto, ON M5B 2K3, Canada * Authors to whom correspondence should be addressed. Appl. Sci. 2026, 16(5), 2216; https://doi.org/10.3390/app16052216 Submission received: 31 December 2025 / Revised: 11 February 2026 / Accepted: 20 February 2026 / Published: 25 February 2026 (This article belongs to the Special Issue Smart Cities: AI-Enhanced Urban Living) Download keyboard_arrow_down Download PDF Download PDF with Cover Download XML Download Epub Browse Figures
Versions Notes Article Views Citations - Abstract The escalating global rates of traffic accidents in urban areas and the growing demands of smart cities underscore the urgent need for advanced real-time monitoring solutions. This paper presents an EdgeML-based system for vehicle tracking that performs real-time speed and distance analysis and traffic violation detection. This is achieved by deploying a YOLOv8 object detection model on a Raspberry Pi 5 with a Coral USB Edge TPU accelerator. The system integrates computer vision and IoT technologies to enable real-time processing. It utilizes the Message Queuing Telemetry Transport (MQTT) protocol to allow scalable communication between distributed edge devices and a central MongoDB database, facilitating real-time storage and analysis of traffic data. A synthetic dataset generated via the Blender 3D modeling tool validates the system's accuracy, demonstrating average speed and distance measurement errors of ±2.11 km/h and ±0.58 m, respectively. These findings are further supported by preliminary practical experiments in a real-world environment, where speed estimation errors remained within 0–2 km/h and distance errors stayed below 0.11 m. Key innovations of this work include license plate recognition, speeding and collision detection, and context analysis using Google's Gemini-2.5-Flash API. A Streamlit dashboard provides real-time visualization of traffic metrics, violations, and aggregated data. A comparative evaluation of YOLOv5n, YOLOv8n, YOLOv11n, and YOLOv12n identifies YOLOv8n as the most suitable model for embedded deployment, achieving 91.07 ± 0.61% mAP@0.5 without quantization, 88.77 ± 3.31% mAP@0.5 with quantization, while maintaining real-time performance of 30–43 frames per second (FPS) on the Edge TPU. The system's modular architecture, low latency, and robust performance highlight its suitability for smart city applications, enhancing traffic safety and enabling data-driven urban mobility management. Keywords: computervision; edge machine learning; Internet of Things; object tracking; public safety; smart cities; traffic management
Introduction Efficient and safe traffic management is a critical challenge worldwide. According to the World Health Organization, road traffic accidents claim approximately 1.19 million lives each year, making them one of the leading causes of death. Children and young adults aged 5 to 29 are among the most affected by traffic accidents. These accidents occur disproportionately in low- and middle-income countries, which account for more than 90% of global traffic fatalities despite owning only about 60% of the world's vehicles [ 1]. These statistics underscore the urgent need for real-time monitoring and enforcement solutions that can mitigate human error and infrastructure shortcomings [ 1, 2]. In many middle- to low-income regions, insufficient investment in road infrastructure, such as poorly marked traffic signs, the absence of pedestrian crossings, and limited surveillance cameras, exacerbates accident risk [ 3]. Drivers and vulnerable road users face heightened danger when clear signage and adequate enforcement of violations, such as speeding or unsafe crossings, are lacking [ 4]. Conversely, intelligent camera systems, when properly integrated with skilled public safety management, have reduced risky behaviors and fatalities [ 5]. Yet, traditional cloud-based approaches introduce latency and privacy concerns, limiting their effectiveness for real-time enforcement [ 6]. The shift toward smart city frameworks demands more agile solutions that can process high volumes of data locally while providing actionable insights for urban mobility [ 7]. Edge machine learning (EdgeML), which brings inference directly to resource-constrained devices at the network's edge, offers a promising solution to the aforementioned issue by reducing latency and enhancing data privacy [ 8, 9, 10]. In parallel, advances in computer vision models, particularly the You Only Look Once (YOLO) family, enable high-speed object detection even on embedded hardware [ 11, 12, 13, 14]. However, optimizing these models for low-power and resource-constrained devices while maintaining accuracy and fast processing remains an open challenge, requiring careful selection of architectures, quantization strategies, and compute accelerators [ 8, 15, 16]. This work addresses the aforementioned challenges by developing and evaluating an EdgeML-driven system for real-time vehicle tracking, traffic flow analysis, and violation detection. By integrating computer vision with IoT technologies, the system enables data-driven urban mobility management, enabling the analysis of congestion patterns and the proactive adjustment of traffic signals. Specifically, we perform comparative tests of YOLOv5n [ 17], YOLOv8n [ 18], YOLOv11n [ 19], and YOLOv12n [ 20], non-quantized, and we compare five deployment formats: float32, float16, int8, ONNX, and EdgeTPU. These models are executed on a Raspberry Pi (RPi) 5 CPU and optionally paired with a Coral USB Edge Tensor Processing Unit (TPU) to identify the optimal trade-off between accuracy and inference speed. After selecting YOLOv8n as the best-performing model, we implement a complete real-time pipeline that estimates vehicle speed and distances between vehicles and between vehicles and pedestrians. The system uses the Message Queuing Telemetry Transport (MQTT) protocol [ 21], ensuring scalable communication between distributed edge devices and a central database, a key requirement for modern smart city infrastructure [ 22]. The system also performs license plate recognition, detects collisions (including vehicle–pedestrian events), and uses Google's Gemini-2.5-Flash API to extract contextual information, such as vehicle type and pedestrian presence, thereby improving traffic infraction classification. In the actual system, video data are acquired from source cameras connected to RPi 5 devices through USB interfaces. The proposed system's modular architecture and robust performance highlight its suitability for smart city applications, directly enhancing public safety. The system is designed for scalable city-wide deployment, with RPi 5 devices installed at key locations such as schools, hospitals, pedestrian crossings, and major roadways. This distributed approach enhances traffic surveillance by enabling real-time monitoring and analysis across multiple points, improving safety and enforcement while maintaining low-latency and edge-based processing. Ultimately, this architecture bridges the gap between local sensing and centralized urban analytics, providing a cost-effective alternative to traditional road sensors while expanding the capabilities of intelligent traffic management systems. The system's source code is publicly available ( https://github.com/HyAgOsK/Traffic-Tracker (accessed on 19 February 2026)). The key contributions of this work are listed next. Model benchmarking on edge hardware: A fair comparison of YOLOv5 to v12 variants under similar training conditions. We benchmark float32, float16, int8, ONNX, and EdgeTPU formats on CPU and Coral Edge TPU, revealing YOLOv8n's as the best overall trade-off, achieving 91.07 ± 0.61% mAP@0.5 and at least 30 FPS on TPU. All reported results are presented as mean ± standard deviation across three training seeds (0, 13, and 42). Real-time EdgeML deployment: Implementation of YOLOv8n integrated with ByteTrack [ 23] on an RPi 5 with optional Coral USB Edge TPU for multi-object tracking [ 24, 25], achieving sustained 30–43 FPS and local computation of speed (with an average error of ±2.11 km/h) and distance (with an average error of ±0.58 m) metrics. Synthetic dataset for validation: Creation of a Blender-generated video corpus, encompassing varied speeds (1–120 km/h), distances (5, 11, 24 m), lighting conditions, and collision scenarios to rigorously quantify system accuracy. Integrated Context-Aware Violation Detection: The system employs an MQTT-based pipeline (using Eclipse Paho MQTT [ 26]) to automate infraction logging, transmitting cropped vehicle images, speed/distance values, timestamps, license plate text, and generative-AI contextual analysis (via Google's Gemini-2.5-flash API) to a centralized MongoDB database [ 27]. This architecture ensures scalable edge-to-cloud communication while enhancing infraction classification through real-time collision risk detection. It calculates distance and speed estimations to identify proximity violations between vehicles and pedestrians. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first traffic monitoring framework to integrate generative AI for real-time scene context and infraction validation. Interactive dashboard: A Streamlit [ 28] application provides real-time visualization of traffic flow, speed violations, collision alerts, and historical analytics, accessible remotely over the network. Therefore, the novelty of this work lies not in proposing new detection or tracking algorithms, but in designing and validating a deployment-oriented EdgeML traffic monitoring system. Unlike prior studies that focus on isolated components or offline evaluation, this work presents a fully integrated, real-time pipeline that jointly performs detection, multi-object tracking, physically calibrated speed and distance estimation, and deterministic infraction detection directly on embedded hardware. By emphasizing end-to-end integration, metric accuracy under resource constraints, and scalable edge deployment, the proposed system advances the practical implementation of vision-based traffic monitoring for smart-city environments. Our proposed system is designed for eventual deployment and real-time frame-processing applications across a smart city environment. However, the current experimental evaluation focuses on a specific scenario with camera configuration, road geometry, and speed range. This evaluation aims to validate the performance of the proposed system in terms of detection speed, vehicle detection, and distance and speed measurements. An eventual deployment of the proposed system in a smart city would require adaptation and further evaluation across different deployment scenarios. Therefore, the conclusions herein should be interpreted primarily for low-speed urban settings. Extensions to high-speed arterials/major roadways require additional evaluation and calibration. In this work, public safety and sustainability are treated as application-level objectives that motivate the proposed system, while the analytical focus is placed on validating the core edge-based perception and measurement capabilities required to support such outcomes. Accordingly, the evaluation emphasizes foundational technical components, including vehicle detection and tracking accuracy, distance and speed measurement fidelity, and real-time edge-processing performance, which constitute necessary enabling conditions for safety- and sustainability-oriented traffic management applications. The direct assessment of higher-level safety and sustainability indicators, such as accident reduction, behavioral change, or environmental impact, requires long-term field deployments, comparative baselines, and policy-contextual analysis, and is therefore beyond the scope of the present study. The remainder of this paper is organized as follows: Section 2 reviews related work in traffic monitoring and edge machine learning, establishing the context and contributions of this research. Subsequently, Section 3 describes the materials and methods, including the training and synthetic datasets, the proposed system architecture, model training and optimization, and the core algorithms for pixel-to-meter transformation, speed, and distance calculation. Section 4 presents and discusses the experimental results, including the comparative performance of YOLO models, the precision of distance and speed measurements, and the implementation of the interactive dashboard. Finally, Section 5 concludes the paper by summarizing the findings and outlining directions for future work.
Related Works Prior research in traffic monitoring has explored various computer vision and machine learning approaches. For instance, in [ 29], the authors used YOLOv4 and Deep Simple Online and Realtime Tracking (DeepSORT), which is a multi-object tracking (MOT) algorithm [ 30], for vehicle detection/counting, tracking, and speed estimation, achieving approximately 80% precision in a relatively small area. Speed measurements resulted in an average error of 3.51 km/h (with a tolerance of ±7 km/h and using a 640-pixel-wide, 360-pixel-high camera). However, the speed measurements were not properly validated, for example, using a dataset created for that purpose, due to the unavailability of annotated video benchmarks. While the method recorded instances of speeding in text blocks, it lacked license plate recognition, distance estimation, collision analysis, and validation datasets. Furthermore, the solution was neither intended nor optimized for edge devices, limiting its real-time applicability in distributed environments. Another approach presented in [ 31] integrated YOLOv4 with DeepSORT and a Gaussian Mixture Model (GMM) to detect road incidents, including vehicles moving in the wrong direction, objects on the road, and vehicles at very high or very low speeds. The system employed the GrabCut algorithm [ 32] for road segmentation. It achieved a mean average precision at an intersection over union (IoU) threshold of 0.5 (mAP@0.5) of 52%, and a detection rate of 10.65 FPS on a server equipped with a graphics processing unit (GPU) (NVIDIA RTX 2080 Ti). Despite its ability to identify stationary vehicles and anomalous speeds, the study reported a high average speed error of ±15 km/h and omitted collision detection. Additionally, speed estimation evaluation used qualitative analysis because annotated video benchmarks were unavailable, undermining reproducibility. The brief literature review presented in [ 33] examines the shift from traditional image processing techniques (e.g., segmentation and filtering) to convolutional neural networks (CNNs) for traffic monitoring. It emphasizes CNNs' ability to automate feature extraction and improve accuracy in tasks like vehicle detection, license plate recognition, and traffic density analysis. Highlighting real-world applications, such as tunnel monitoring systems using deep belief networks and real-time tracking frameworks like SiamFC, the review underscored the cost-effectiveness and scalability of camera-based systems over traditional road sensors. It concluded that CNNs outperform conventional methods in dynamic environments, advocating the future integration of multimodal data (e.g., autonomous vehicular data) and expansion into broader domains, such as pedestrian behavior analysis, to ultimately enhance urban safety and traffic efficiency. In [ 34], a highway speed detection system combining YOLOv5s with DeepSORT was presented. The YOLOv5s model was customized with the Swin Transformer block and Complete Intersection over Union (CIoU) loss function, which enhanced its precision and convergence speed. DeepSORT was optimized to track occluded vehicles using techniques such as the Mahalanobis metric and cosine distance. It achieved an mAP@0.5 exceeding 90%, an F1-score of 97%, and an absolute speed error of ±1–8 km/h. However, the solution did not address collision scenarios or license plate recognition and relied on GPU-based hardware (RTX 3080 Ti), which is unsuitable for edge deployment. Additionally, no dataset was made available for validating the speed estimation. Broader applications of computer vision in traffic management have also been explored. The review in [ 35] proposes an intelligent system to optimize traffic flow using computer vision techniques, such as object detection and tracking. Such techniques can process historical and live data to predict congestion patterns, enabling proactive traffic management and data-driven infrastructure planning. By analyzing real-time data from strategically placed cameras, the suggested system dynamically adjusts traffic signals and lane assignments, detects incidents (e.g., accidents, obstacles), and enforces traffic laws through automated violation detection (e.g., red-light running, illegal lane changes). However, it lacks a concrete implementation of the suggested system, proposes no deep learning architectures for the traffic management problem, and provides no empirical validation of speed or collision metrics. The authors of [ 36] developed a road feature detection system for Advanced Driver Assistance Systems (ADAS) using a dual-module deep learning approach. The system utilized YOLOv7 for traffic type identification and Faster R-CNN for traffic sign recognition. Trained on an NVIDIA Tesla P100 GPU using Google Colab, the YOLOv7 model achieved an mAP@0.5 of 87.21%, while the Faster R-CNN achieved 75.64%. Although effective at feature detection, the study did not include speed measurement or collision detection, which may impact its immediate application in real-time edge safety systems. Table 1 compares the related works with the system proposed in this paper. It is important to note that when interpreting the comparative data in the table, it is essential to acknowledge that the referenced studies were conducted under significantly different experimental conditions, encompassing diverse datasets, camera configurations, evaluation protocols, and hardware architectures. These inherent methodological and environmental discrepancies, which also make direct metrics like FPS, CPU, and RAM usage difficult to compare, render a strict quantitative head-to-head benchmark less meaningful. Consequently, the table utilizes multiple columns to indicate the presence or absence of specific technical capabilities, serving as a qualitative comparison of the functional and deployment scopes of different research efforts rather than a purely numerical performance ranking. Unlike other work in the literature, results for our benchmarked models are reported as mean ± standard deviation across three seeds (0, 13, and 42). Table 1. Comparison of related works with the proposed system. The comparative analysis demonstrates that our proposed system distinguishes itself from existing approaches by employing YOLOv8n and ByteTrack for real-time traffic monitoring, achieving a balanced trade-off between speed and distance measurement errors. Unlike prior approaches, our solution uniquely supports collision detection, license plate recognition, generative AI-based contextual analysis, and edge deployment on an RPi 5 with Edge TPU, delivering 30–43 FPS on an RPi 5 with an Edge TPU under the reported embedded configuration. Rigorously validated on a synthetic Blender-generated dataset and further supported by preliminary real-world experiments, our system addresses key gaps in prior work by emphasizing end-to-end integration and deployability, offering a practical and efficient solution for intelligent traffic management. Additionally, Table 1 shows that prior studies typically report isolated components (e.g., detection, tracking, or speed estimation) but seldom provide a deployment-oriented, end-to-end solution that maintains real-time, frame-level processing under embedded constraints while producing actionable traffic-control outputs. Moreover, none of the reviewed works utilize generative AI for contextual infraction description. Therefore, the central contribution of this work is an integrated EdgeML pipeline that unifies detection and tracking with calibrated physical-metric estimation and structured infraction logging, enabling practical smart-city operation from on-device inference through communication, storage, and visualization.
Materials and Methods This section presents the architecture and processing pipeline of the proposed edge-based traffic monitoring system, which integrates object detection, multi-object tracking, spatial calibration, speed and distance estimation, and rule-based infraction analysis into a real-time frame-processing pipeline for embedded deployment. In this work, real-time performance refers to the edge device's ability to process incoming video frames at a sustained rate without a backlog in the core analytics pipeline. The methodology builds on established techniques from computer vision, signal processing, and kinematics to ensure robustness and reproducibility, while implementation details and parameter values are reported in the context of the evaluated deployment scenario. The pseudo-code formulations describe the system's conceptual logic and support reproducibility; numerical values shown correspond to the experimental setup and represent configurable operational parameters rather than fixed design choices. 3.1. Real-World Dataset The dataset used for model training was created by combining and curating multiple real-world datasets available on Roboflow [ 37], resulting in a unified, publicly available database [ 38]. It comprises six classes—cars (56,556), motorcycles (23,926), vans (5409), people (43,533), trucks (19,587), and buses (16,219)—totaling 165,230 object instances across 82,008 images. The dataset was divided into 80% for training and 20% for validation during parameter tuning. All images were auto-oriented and stretched to a resolution of 640 × 640 pixels. No data augmentation was applied to the training set. The dataset distribution is illustrated in Figure 1. Figure 1. Dataset distribution across six classes. The vertical axis indicates the number of images per class, while the horizontal axis shows the target classes. 3.2. Blender-Generated Synthetic Dataset A synthetic, publicly available [ 39] video dataset was created using Blender [ 40], a 3D modeling tool, to evaluate the model's performance in a controlled, near-realistic environment. The dataset contains 1920 × 1080-pixel videos that enable vehicle-tracking assessment across various conditions, eliminating the limitations and variability of real-world data and ensuring a standardized scenario for more precise analyses. The database is modular and adjustable, allowing vehicle speeds and distances to be customized as needed. It is divided into two video categories, 60 FPS and 30 FPS, facilitating model evaluation at different capture rates. The videos simulate vehicles moving on a highway at speeds ranging from 1 to 120 km/h, with fixed distances between cars of 5 m, 11 m, and 24 m. The simulated highway is 10 m wide and 100 m long. Additionally, simulations include license plates for character recognition testing. Specific test scenarios were designed to evaluate the model's robustness, incorporating variations in lighting conditions (ranging from bright to low-light environments) and simulations of vehicle-to-vehicle and vehicle-to-pedestrian collisions. In the pedestrian collision scenario, the vehicle was positioned 13 m from the pedestrian and advanced at a constant speed of 5 km/h. This structured framework enables controlled performance assessment of the model before real-world deployment. 3.3. System Architecture The proposed system employs edge computing devices for real-time vehicle detection and tracking at key locations. Each edge device is connected to a USB camera and operates either standalone or in conjunction with an Edge TPU accelerator. Using the accelerator can significantly improve inference speed and efficiency. All image processing is performed locally on the device, enabling data privacy and security and low-latency responses without relying on the cloud. In this study, an RPi 5 Model B connected to a 720p (1280 × 720 pixels) USB camera is used as the edge computing device. It can be optionally connected to a Coral USB Edge TPU for improved performance. The system architecture is depicted in Figure 2, detailing its main components and data flow. Figure 2. Proposed system architecture. For tracking objects and traffic control, the system integrates the YOLOv8 model, selected through the comparative analysis presented in Section 4, due to its balance between accuracy and computational efficiency. ByteTrack, a robust data association algorithm that ensures temporal consistency of tracked objects, handles object tracking. This architecture enables the automatic identification of traffic violations and the capture of relevant frames for further analysis. The system functions with or without the Edge TPU. When operating solely on the RPi 5, inference is performed entirely on the CPU, allowing for autonomous deployment and lower costs, albeit at lower frame rates. With the Edge TPU, inference performance improves significantly, enabling higher frame rates and making it suitable for end-to-end real-time frame-level traffic monitoring on embedded edge devices. Two dedicated modules running on the edge devices assess traffic violations, speeding, and collisions. They monitor speed limits and detect potential collisions involving vehicles and pedestrians. The system uses the MQTT protocol to transmit data from each RPi 5 to a centralized server, enabling scalable, distributed deployment. This server also functions as the MQTT broker and stores the received data in a MongoDB database for future analysis. The video feed captured by each RPi 5, along with historical analytics generated from processing and analyzing the feed, is transmitted directly to a Streamlit-based dashboard. This edge-centric approach minimizes latency by eliminating intermediaries, enabling data to flow directly from the device to the dashboard. Video feed processing and analysis occur at one-minute intervals at the edge device, aggregating metrics such as vehicle speeds, inter-vehicle and vehicle-to-pedestrian distances, violation counts, and traffic/pedestrian flow (including the number of vehicles and people) within each interval. After aggregating the metrics, they are sent to the server for storage in a centralized MongoDB database. Traffic violations, such as speeding or collisions, trigger a visual alert on the dashboard and are stored in the MongoDB database. The recorded information includes the type of violation (collision or speeding), date and time of the event, vehicle tracking identifier (ID), distance between cars and car to pedestrian, speed, multi-modal context of the violation (obtained via Gemini-2.5flash) [ 41], license plate, object count (traffic flow), captured image filename, and the cumulative number of violations recorded up to that point. All the gathered information is also stored on the edge device to ensure redundancy. The generative AI component operates strictly as a post hoc contextualization layer and does not participate in infraction detection or decision-making. All infractions are identified exclusively through deterministic, metric-based rules derived from calibrated speed, distance, trajectory, and spatial measurements. Once a violation, such as speeding or a collision risk, is confirmed by the rule-based analytics, the generative model is invoked solely to enrich the event with high-level semantic information, including vehicle attributes and auxiliary descriptive cues such as license plate text recognition. By limiting its role to contextual description and interpretability enhancement, the generative AI module does not influence or override the underlying deterministic analytics, thereby preserving the transparency, reproducibility, and metric-driven nature of the core processing pipeline. The dashboard retrieves data directly from each RPi 5 on the network, using its unique IP address, to enable real-time information visualization. This unique IP association enables the system to link each event to its source device. To access a specific edge device on the network from a dashboard, it is only necessary to enter the device's IP address to view the information it processes. 3.4. Tracking Models The established YOLO variants, YOLOv5 and YOLOv8, along with recent implementations released as open-source software or preprints, YOLOv11 and YOLOv12, models in their nano size, were selected for this work because they prioritize speed, low resource usage, and edge–device deployability, making them ideal for smart traffic systems, drones, and other embedded AI applications. Although not formally peer-reviewed, YOLOv11 and YOLOv12 were included in this study as empirical benchmarks to assess the practical performance of recent object detection implementations on resource-constrained edge hardware. The evolution of YOLO models reflects a consistent focus on optimizing single-stage object detection architectures for efficiency. In this sense, YOLOv5 [ 17], one of the first widely used state-of-the-art single-stage object detectors, employs a CSPDarknet backbone [ 42], a PANet neck [ 43] for feature extraction and fusion, and anchor-based detection with grid prediction. The CSPDarknet backbone efficiently extracts key visual features while reducing computational redundancy, and the PANet neck combines information across multiple resolution levels to accurately detect objects of different sizes. The anchor-based grid prediction effectively localizes and sizes objects in the image [ 42]. Key innovations include mosaic augmentation and auto-learned anchor boxes. Its most prominent successor, YOLOv8, enhances the backbone architecture and adopts an anchor-free head that directly predicts object centers. It introduces a Task-Aligned Assigner to improve label assignment and distribution, achieving a superior speed–accuracy trade-off via focal loss [ 18]. The next widely used version, YOLOv11 [ 19], introduces a cross-stage partial block with kernel size 2 (C3K2), replacing YOLOv8's cross-stage partial with dual feature connections (C2f) modules, and uses smaller 3 × 3 kernels to improve efficiency. The cross-stage partial with polarized self-attention (C2PSA) attention mechanism is another highlight, enhancing spatial focus for small objects. The Spatial Pyramid Pooling Fast (SPPF) module handles multi-scale features and demonstrates notable multi-task versatility across detection, segmentation, and pose estimation. One of the latest versions, YOLOv12 [ 20], introduces the area attention ( A 2 ) module, which segments feature maps to reduce computational cost while preserving receptive fields. The residual efficient layer aggregation networks (R-ELAN) architecture improves feature fusion with residual connections, and FlashAttention (a memory-efficient attention mechanism optimized for GPU use) optimizes memory access. Key architectural tweaks include removing positional encoding and reducing the multi-layer perceptron (MLP) expansion ratio, which sets the hidden-layer width relative to the input channels, thereby lowering computational cost and parameter count. 3.5. Training and Optimization of the Models To ensure reproducibility and a fair comparison between models, the training of YOLOv5n, YOLOv8n, YOLOv11n, and YOLOv12n was conducted with the same value for the hyperparameters shown in Table 2. This approach ensured consistent evaluation of performance metrics, eliminating potential training variations and allowing observed differences in results to be attributed exclusively to the model architectures. Furthermore, to optimize training, transfer learning (i.e., fine-tuning) was employed using pre-trained weights from all four models, providing an effective initialization since the pre-trained models already recognize common objects such as vehicles and people, ensuring more consistent detection. All models were pre-trained on the MS-COCO dataset [ 44] and fine-tuned on the real-world dataset described in Section 3.1. Table 2. Training hyperparameters used for all YOLO models. To account for the stochastic nature of deep learning optimization, each YOLO variant was trained in N = 3 independent runs using different random seeds (0, 13, and 42). In each run, the dataset was reshuffled and re-split into 80% training and 20% validation according to the corresponding seed, while keeping all hyperparameters fixed in Table 2. Detection performance is reported as mean and ± standard deviation across these runs. The training process was structured for up to 5000 epochs, with an early stopping patience of 500 epochs. To optimize data processing during training, automatic batch sizes were employed, allowing YOLO to determine the optimal batch size automatically based on the GPU's capabilities. Additionally, the cache argument was enabled to reduce the time the GPU spends waiting for data to be loaded from the disk, optimizing data loading and scheduling throughout the training process. All models, using pre-training weights, were initially trained and stored in the native PyTorch (version 1.8 or higher, with Python 3.8+; accessed on 19 February 2026) format (.pt). After training, each model was exported and quantized into float32, float16 and int8 formats, targeting three deployment-optimized runtimes: TFLite (TensorFlow Lite) (version 2.16.2+; accessed on 19 February 2026): Google's lightweight runtime for mobile/edge devices, enabling low-latency inference with hardware acceleration via delegates (e.g., GPU, Hexagon) [ 45]. EdgeTPU (version 16.0; accessed on 19 February 2026): Hardware-specific format for Google's Coral TPU hardware, requiring full-integer (int8) quantization to maximize throughput on embedded accelerators [ 46]. ONNX (Open Neural Network Exchange) (version 1.17.0; accessed on 19 February 2026): Framework-agnostic format executed via ONNX Runtime (version 1.20.1; accessed on 19 February 2026), supporting cross-platform CPU/GPU acceleration through execution providers [ 47]. This optimization reduces model size and computational complexity, enabling faster processing and higher FPS, but may result in a performance reduction. During export to these formats, resolutions of 640 × 640 (default), 260 × 260, and 192 × 192 pixels were evaluated to determine the ideal balance between speed and accuracy. Lower resolutions increase frame rates at the expense of reduced detection quality. 3.6. Pixel-to-Meter Transformation To estimate a vehicle's real-world position from image data, it is necessary to convert pixel coordinates to metric coordinates. This conversion is achieved by a planar projective transformation, commonly known as a homography [ 48], which establishes a geometric relationship between the image plane and the ground plane. The planar homography is a standard projective geometry technique widely used to map image-plane coordinates to real-world ground–plane coordinates in traffic surveillance and camera calibration [ 48]. The process begins by defining a region of interest (ROI) in the image, corresponding to the portion of the scene where vehicle detection and tracking are performed (e.g., the roadway). From this ROI, four non-collinear reference points are selected, and their pixel coordinates are organized into a 4 × 2 matrix referred to as the SOURCE matrix. A second 4 × 2 matrix, denoted as the TARGET matrix, contains the corresponding real-world coordinates of the same reference points, measured in meters on the ground plane. These coordinates must be expressed in a consistent metric reference system. The pairing of SOURCE and TARGET points enables the estimation of the homography matrix, as described in [ 49]. Once the reference correspondences are defined, a standard homographic transformation is computed, resulting in a 3 × 3 transformation matrix, H [ 48, 49, 50]. This matrix maps any pixel coordinate within the calibrated ROI to its corresponding position in real-world metric coordinates. In vehicle tracking, the transformation is typically applied to the bottom-center point of each detected bounding box (bbox), providing a better approximation of the vehicle's contact point with the ground. Algorithm 1 summarizes the procedure used to estimate the homography matrix and to perform the pixel-to-meter coordinate transformation. Algorithm 1 Pixel-to-meter transformation using homography 1: function InitializeViewTransformer( s o u r c e , t a r g e t ) 2: H ← ComputeHomography ( ) ( s o u r c e , t a r g e t ) 3: return H 4: end function 5: function TransformPixelToMeter( p o i n t s p x , H ) 6: if p o i n t s p x = ∅ or H is undefined then 7: return p o i n t s p x 8: end if 9: p o i n t s m t ← ApplyPerspectiveTransform() ( p o i n t s p x , H ) 10: return p o i n t s m t 11: end function Figure 3 illustrates an ROI example of a physical deployment where detections and tracking will take place. In this region, pixel values are transformed into meters using Algorithm 1. The ROI is a rectangle measuring 30 m × 6.6 m. The corresponding pixel coordinates for the vertices of the rectangle are also shown, considering a video resolution of 1280 × 720 pixels. Figure 3. Example of an ROI in a real scenario, illustrating the spatial dimensions in both pixels and meters. Figure 4 illustrates the pixels-to-meter transformation applied to a Blender-generated synthetic video. The analysis includes two distinct perspectives: a lateral, tangential view of the track and a top-down view. Each post on the same side of the road in the figure is 25 m apart and 10 m apart from the others on the other side of the road. The figure contains two images: the first shows a rectangle with dimensions 100 m × 10 m, and the second shows a rectangle with dimensions 25 m × 10 m. Figure 4. Simulated vehicle detection areas created in Blender to validate distance and speed measurements, showing the corresponding dimensions in pixels and meters. To align the synthetic evaluation with the practical operational setting, the Blender scene geometry and ROI configuration were defined based on measurements from the real deployment camera setup shown in Figure 3. This real-to-synthetic alignment guided the choice of the synthetic speed and distance configurations so that they mirror typical real-world situations while still providing controlled ground-truth conditions. 3.7. Traffic Flow Calculation The traffic flow is defined as the number of new objects entering the polygonal ROI within a fixed time window, in line with prior vision-based traffic flow estimation that relies on vehicle counting over a fixed time window [ 51]. Each tracked object ID is counted only once, at the first frame in which its centroid lies inside the ROI. The dashboard updates the flow every minute by recomputing the flow over the last 60 s, as shown below. The NO denotes the number of objects that entered the ROI during the time window, and t ( min ) is the window duration in minutes (min). flow = NO t ( min ) [ number of objects / minute ] (1) 3.8. Speed Calculation The vehicle speed estimation process is performed in several well-defined stages that combine geometric transformation, object detection and tracking, temporal analysis, and signal smoothing. The overall objective is to obtain a reliable, stable estimate of the instantaneous vehicle speed as it moves within a predefined ROI. Vehicle speed is computed using the classical kinematic definition of velocity as displacement over time, a formulation commonly adopted in vision-based traffic monitoring systems [ 52]. Initially, a homography-based transformation matrix is computed using Algorithm 1. This matrix establishes a mapping between image-plane pixel coordinates and real-world metric coordinates, enabling accurate conversion of pixel-based distances to meters. This step is essential to ensure that subsequent speed calculations are physically meaningful. For each video frame, the system continuously evaluates whether an object of interest, either a vehicle or a pedestrian, enters the predefined polygonal ROI. Object detection is carried out using the YOLO framework, while temporal association and identity preservation across frames are achieved through the robust ByteTrack multi-object tracking algorithm [ 23]. Speed estimation within the end-to-end detection and tracking pipeline follows the reference algorithm adopted in this work. The Supervision library [ 53] is employed to manage and synchronize the integration of detection, tracking, and visualization components. The underlying speed estimation algorithm is based on computing the average velocity of each tracked object by analyzing its displacement over a given time interval, specifically by dividing the difference in spatial position (measured in real-world coordinates) by the corresponding difference in time between observations. More specifically, within this pipeline, speed estimation is performed by first transforming pixel coordinates into real-world metric coordinates using a homography-based planar projective transformation. Following this conversion, the algorithm calculates the average speed by determining the displacement between the initial and final tracked coordinates of an object relative to the time interval, which is derived from the system's frame rate. Finally, a standard Exponential Moving Average (EMA) [ 54] is applied to the calculated values to smooth the speed estimates and ensure temporal stability across frames. EMA filtering is a standard time-series smoothing technique widely used in signal processing and tracking applications [ 54]. Tracking is performed using the bottom-center (BC) point of each detected bbox. This reference point is chosen because it provides a more stable approximation of the object's contact point with the ground plane, which is particularly important for accurate distance estimation in traffic monitoring scenarios. As the object moves within the ROI, its BC coordinates are recorded at each frame and stored in a list associated with the object's unique ID. This list contains the sequence of metric coordinates over time for each tracked object. The distance traveled by a vehicle is computed as the absolute difference between its current and previous coordinates in the transformed (meter-based) space. Letting these coordinates represent consecutive positions along the trajectory, the cumulative displacement can be estimated directly from their difference. The elapsed time corresponding to this displacement is determined from the number of stored coordinate samples and the video frame rate. Specifically, if N frames are associated with a given object trajectory and the video has a frame rate of video_fps, the elapsed time is given by N/video_fps seconds. Using these quantities, the instantaneous vehicle speed is calculated according to ( 2). Since the distance is measured in meters and the time in seconds, the result is initially expressed in meters per second and then converted to kilometers per hour by multiplying by 3.6. speed = distance time × 3.6 [ km / h ] . (2) To reduce the effect of measurement noise and small frame-to-frame variations in the detected positions, an EMA is applied to the sequence of speed estimates [ 55]. This smoothing process is defined by ( 3), where more recent speed measurements are assigned higher weights, while older observations contribute progressively less to the final value. EMA t = α · speed t + ( 1 − α ) · EMA t − 1 km / h , (3) The smoothing factor a l p h a ∈ [ 0 , 1 ] controls the responsiveness of the EMA. Larger values of α emphasize recent observations, making the filtered speed more sensitive to sudden changes. In contrast, smaller values give greater weight to past observations, producing a smoother, more stable speed profile. In the experimental evaluation, the smoothing factor α was set to 0.5 as a representative trade-off between responsiveness and stability, ensuring that the estimated speed reacts adequately to genuine changes while suppressing short-term fluctuations. However, this parameter is configurable and may be adapted to different noise characteristics or motion dynamics. Algorithm 2 summarizes the complete speed estimation procedure. The algorithm iterates over all detected object IDs, checks whether historical coordinate data are available, computes the traveled distance and elapsed time, and then evaluates the instantaneous speed. When available, the previous EMA value is used to update the smoothed speed estimate, which is subsequently associated with the corresponding object ID for visualization. Once a vehicle is detected within the ROI, it is assigned a unique ID, and its initial and final positions are used to estimate the distance traveled over the observation interval. Based on the camera frame rate or real-time video input, the elapsed time is computed, and the vehicle speed is obtained using the velocity equation in ( 2). The EMA-filtered speed is then converted to a textual label and overlaid on the corresponding bbox, ensuring a clear, intuitive visualization of vehicle motion within the traffic monitoring system. Algorithm 2 Speed calculation Require: Smoothing factor, α 1: function CalculateEMA( e m a t − 1 , s p e e d ) 2: return α · s p e e d + ( 1 − α ) · e m a t − 1 3: end function 4: function CalculateSpeed( l a b e l s , d e t e c t i o n s , c o o r d i n a t e s , f p s ) 5: for each i d ∈ d e t e c t i o n s do 6: if i d ∉ c o o r d i n a t e s then 7: Append( l a b e l s , i d ) 8: else 9: p l a s t ← Last( c o o r d i n a t e s [ i d ] ) 10: p f i r s t ← First( c o o r d i n a t e s [ i d ] ) 11: d ← | p l a s t − p f i r s t | 12: t ← Length( c o o r d i n a t e s [ i d ] ) / f p s 13: s p e e d ← 3.6 · d / t 14: if e m a t − 1 is undefined then 15: e m a t − 1 ← s p e e d 16: else 17: e m a ← CalculateEMA( e m a t − 1 , s p e e d ) 18: e m a t − 1 ← e m a 19: id_str ← ConvertToString(id) 20: Append( l a b e l s , i d _ s t r , e m a ) 21: end if 22: end if 23: end for 24: end function 3.9. Distance Calculation The distance between two vehicles, or between a vehicle and a pedestrian, is computed using the Euclidean distance, which represents the standard geometric distance between two points in a two-dimensional metric space and is commonly employed in computer vision and object tracking [ 56]. For each detected and tracked object, a representative reference point is defined as the BC of its bbox. These BC points correspond to the physical contact point of the object with the ground plane and have already been mapped to real-world coordinates through the homography transformation described in Algorithm 1. The distance between two objects is obtained by computing the straight-line distance between their respective BC points. When more than two objects are present in the same frame, pairwise distances are calculated for all object combinations, and the minimum distance is selected as the most critical (i.e., smallest) separation. This strategy ensures that the closest interactions between objects are properly identified and monitored. The Euclidean distance between two points p A = ( p A x , p A y ) and p B = ( p B x , p B y ) in the two-dimensional plane is given by d = ( p A x − p B x ) 2 + ( p A y − p B y ) 2 [ m ] , (4) where the resulting value represents the physical distance between the objects in meters. Algorithm 3 illustrates the procedure used to compute and visualize this distance. It consists of two main functions. The first function, EuclideanDistance, takes two points as input and computes their distance using ( 4). The second function, AnnotateDistance, provides visual feedback by drawing a line connecting the two points in the video frame. It overlays the numerical distance value at the midpoint of the line. This visual annotation facilitates real-time interpretation of spatial relationships between tracked objects. Algorithm 3 Distance calculation and annotation 1: function EuclideanDistance( p A , p B ) 2: return ( p A x − p B x ) 2 + ( p A y − p B y ) 2 3: end function 4: function AnnotateDistance( f r a m e , p A , p B , d ) 5: DrawLine( f r a m e , p A , p B ) 6: p m i d ← p A x + p B x 2 , p A y + p B y 2 7: DrawText( f r a m e , d , p m i d ) 8: return f r a m e 9: end function 3.10. Edge AI-Driven Traffic Infraction Detection and MQTT Data Pipeline Traffic infractions are detected at the edge locations whenever predefined safety conditions are violated. Specifically, an infraction is triggered when a vehicle exceeds the configured road speed limit or when the distance between vehicles or between a vehicle and a pedestrian falls below a minimum safety threshold. In the experimental setup, the speed threshold was set to 30 km/h, and the minimum safe distance was set to 4 m. The speed and distance thresholds used in this study reflect the evaluated urban scenario, local traffic regulations, and application context. These parameters are treated as configurable system settings rather than fixed design constraints and may be adjusted to accommodate different road types, safety policies, or deployment conditions. To avoid false positives, proximity violations are evaluated only when the involved vehicle is in motion. Stationary vehicles are excluded by continuously analyzing their average speed over time. Once an infraction is detected, Algorithm 4 is executed on the corresponding RPi 5 edge device. The algorithm receives as input the vehicle bbox, tracking ID, estimated speed, measured distance, object count, and the current video frame. The vehicle region is cropped from the frame and stored locally, ensuring visual evidence of the event. At the same time, a structured record is created containing key metadata, including the infraction type (e.g., speeding or collision risk), timestamp, vehicle speed, inter-object distance, traffic and pedestrian flow, and the vehicle identifier. To enrich low-level detection with semantic understanding, the system integrates a higher-level contextual analysis stage using the Gemini-2.5-Flash API. Acting as a virtual traffic safety analyst, the Gemini model processes the cropped vehicle image to extract contextual information such as vehicle type and color, pedestrian presence, license plate text, and overall scene characteristics. The detected infraction type is explicitly forwarded to the model, enabling it to reason about the violation and its surrounding context jointly. For example, if a collision or near-collision follows a speeding event, both conditions are analyzed together. Otherwise, isolated infractions are evaluated independently. This context-aware reasoning improves infraction validation and classification, increasing robustness in complex traffic scenarios. After the contextual analysis, the LogInfractionAndPublish function consolidates all collected information into a JSON payload. Each RPi 5 operates as an MQTT client, publishing the payload to the project/inference topic. For reliability, the same information is also written to a local text-based log file, providing redundancy and fault tolerance in case of network disruptions. The MQTT broker forwards incoming messages to a centralized MongoDB database, where infractions are persistently stored for auditing, traceability, and further analysis by traffic authorities. Algorithm 4 Client-side infraction handling and data publishing logic (illustrative configuration) 1: function LogInfractionAndPublish( b b o x , i d , f r a m e , s p e e d , n _ i n f , d i s t a n c e , n _ o b j ) 2: ( x 1 , y 1 , x 2 , y 2 ) ← ExtractBoundingBox( b b o x ) 3: v e h i c l e ← CropImage( f r a m e , x 1 , y 1 , x 2 , y 2 ) 4: i m g P a t h ← SaveImage( v e h i c l e ) 5: a i M o d e l ← ConfigureVisionLanguageModel( A P I _ K E Y ) 6: d e s c r i p t i o n ← AnalyzeImage( a i M o d e l , i m g P a t h ) 7: d e t a i l s ← CreateRecord 8: AddField( d e t a i l s , “infraction_type”, InfractionType) 9: AddField( d e t a i l s , “timestamp”, CurrentTime) 10: AddField( d e t a i l s , “vehicle_id”, id) 11: AddField( d e t a i l s , “distance”, distance) 12: AddField( d e t a i l s , “speed”, speed) 13: AddField( d e t a i l s , “context_and_plate”, description) 14: AddField( d e t a i l s , “image”, imgPath) 15: AddField( d e t a i l s , “num_objects”, n_obj) 16: AddField( d e t a i l s , “infractions”, n_inf) 17: WriteLog d e t a i l s 18: c l i e n t ← InitializeMQTTClient 19: Connect( c l i e n t , BrokerAddress, 1883 ) 20: Subscribe( c l i e n t , “project/inference”) 21: p a y l o a d ← EncodeJSON( d e t a i l s ) 22: Publish( c l i e n t , “project/inference”, payload) 23: Disconnect( c l i e n t ) 24: end function On the server side, Algorithm 5 manages data ingestion and storage. The server initializes an MQTT client, connects to the database, and subscribes to the project/inference topic. Incoming messages are decoded from JSON format and directly inserted into the MongoDB collection. This publish–subscribe architecture enables real-time, asynchronous, and scalable data aggregation from multiple distributed edge devices, supporting downstream applications such as violation logging, dashboard visualization, and urban mobility analytics. Continuous operation is ensured through the MQTT event loop, allowing uninterrupted monitoring and storage of traffic infraction data. Algorithm 5 Server – MQTT subscriber and database storage 1: function StartServer 2: c l i e n t ← InitializeMQTTClient 3: d a t a b a s e ← ConnectToDatabase 4: SubscribeToTopic( c l i e n t , “project/inference”) 5: while server is running do 6: m s g ← ReceiveMessage( c l i e n t ) 7: if m s g ≠ ∅ then 8: d a t a ← DecodePayload( m s g ) 9: Store( d a t a b a s e , d a t a ) 10: end if 11: end while 12: end function The proposed intelligent traffic monitoring system is organized around Algorithm 6, which orchestrates a set of modular processing stages. These stages include homography-based coordinate transformation (Algorithm 1), vehicle and pedestrian speed estimation (Algorithm 2), inter-object distance computation (Algorithm 3), and contextual license plate recognition. The system also integrates asynchronous communication via the MQTT protocol, where RPi 5 devices operate as edge clients (Algorithm 4) and a centralized server receives and persists data in a cloud-based MongoDB database (Algorithm 5). This modular, distributed pipeline enables real-time processing of video streams to detect and track road users, evaluate traffic violations, and transmit structured alerts within a scalable edge–cloud architecture. At the core of the pipeline, image-plane coordinates are mapped into a metric space using the homography matrix, H , enabling accurate spatial measurements in meters. Vehicles and pedestrians are detected using a YOLO object-tracking model optimized for edge inference, and temporally associated across frames using the ByteTrack tracking algorithm. To limit the analysis to relevant regions, a predefined PolygonZone is applied. For each tracked object, a history of transformed positions is maintained, enabling speed estimation from frame-to-frame displacement and the video frame rate. The resulting velocity estimates are smoothed using the EMA estimator defined in Algorithm 2, following ( 3). In parallel, pairwise Euclidean distances between objects are computed to identify unsafe proximity situations or potential collision risks. When a traffic violation is detected, either due to excessive speed or insufficient distance between vehicles or pedestrians, the system invokes the LogInfractionAndPublish function. This module aggregates contextual information related to the event, including the annotated vehicle image, the detected license plate, a timestamp, object identifiers, and the total number of detected entities. License plate recognition and contextual scene understanding are performed using the Gemini-2.5-Flash API model, which also extracts additional visual cues related to the traffic event and motion patterns. The collected data are encapsulated in a JSON message and transmitted to the server via MQTT. On the server side, messages published to the “project/inference” topic are received and stored in the cloud-hosted MongoDB database. The stored records are later queried using the unique identifier of each RPi 5 edge device and visualized through the Streamlit-based dashboard. This interface provides real-time access to key indicators, including vehicle speeds, inter-object distances, recognized license plates, and contextual metadata, supporting informed decision-making for traffic monitoring and enforcement. As illustrated in Figure 5, the proposed architecture tightly integrates edge AI, real-time communication, and cloud-based storage to enable a low-latency, robust processing flow, making it well-suited for smart city deployments and intelligent traffic control systems. EdgeML processing flow diagram for vehicle tracking and traffic control system.
Results and Discussions All object detection and tracking models were trained on a high-performance workstation equipped with an Intel i7-14700KF processor, 128 GB of RAM, and an NVIDIA RTX 4090 GPU. 4.1. Comparison of Object Tracking Models This subsection presents a comprehensive analysis of the experimental results, comparing the performance of YOLOv5, YOLOv8, YOLOv11, and YOLOv12 under identical training conditions. To ensure a fair and reproducible evaluation, all object detection models were trained and validated using the real-world dataset described in Section 3.1, and the same hyperparameter configuration (see Table 2) and random seeds, allowing observed performance differences to be attributed solely to architectural design choices. The evaluation considers standard detection metrics, including mean Average Precision at an IoU threshold of 0.5 (mAP), precision (P), recall (R), and F1-score (F1). Precision measures the proportion of correct positive detections among all predicted positives, while recall quantifies the proportion of correctly detected objects relative to all ground-truth instances. The F1-score provides a balanced measure that jointly reflects precision and recall. The reported detection metrics are computed on the 20% validation subset. Beyond detection accuracy, the analysis also focuses on computational efficiency, which is critical for real-time edge deployment. Performance is therefore assessed based on CPU utilization, RAM usage, and inference throughput, measured in frames per second (FPS). The FPS values reported in this section are measured on the edge device (Raspberry Pi with Edge TPU acceleration) and correspond to the core on-device processing loop. This loop includes object detection inference, multi-object tracking updates, homography-based pixel-to-meter transformation, per-frame metric computation (e.g., speed and distance estimation), and the generation and publication of MQTT messages. Downstream components, including database insertion and dashboard visualization, operate outside the critical processing path and are therefore excluded from the FPS measurement. Accordingly, FPS is used as an indicator of frame-level real-time suitability, with detection and tracking constituting the dominant computational stages. Additionally, model size is reported for different deployment formats and quantization strategies, namely float32, float16, int8, ONNX, and EdgeTPU. All experiments were conducted on an RPi 5, both using the CPU alone and in combination with a Coral USB Edge TPU accelerator. This setup enables a detailed evaluation of how quantization and hardware acceleration affect inference speed, resource usage, and overall system performance. From a practical standpoint, model quantization plays a key role in enabling real-time inference on embedded platforms. By reducing numerical precision, quantization reduces memory footprint and computational complexity, resulting in higher FPS and lower power consumption, which are essential characteristics for edge devices such as the RPi 5 and Edge TPU. However, these benefits typically come at the cost of reduced detection accuracy, highlighting an inherent trade-off between computational efficiency and predictive performance. Higher inference rates directly translate into smoother and more responsive real-time tracking, which is particularly important for latency-sensitive applications such as traffic monitoring, collision risk assessment, and autonomous or semi-autonomous systems. Unless otherwise stated, resource metrics (Size/FPS/CPU/RAM) are reported as point estimates measured on the RPi 5 under a fixed runtime configuration. In contrast, to assess robustness to data partitioning and training randomness, we repeated the full training procedure across three seeds (0, 13, and 42), each time reshuffling and re-splitting the dataset using the corresponding seed, and we report the mean and standard deviation of the detection metrics across these N = 3 different runs. Table 3, Table 4, Table 5 and Table 6 summarize the mean ± standard deviation of mAP@0.5, P, R and F1 across the three seeds and resource utilization of the models for the baseline 640 × 640, 260 × 260, and 192 × 192 configurations. Table 3. Performance of YOLOv5n with different quantization types and image resolutions on RPi 5. Table 4. Performance of YOLOv8n with different quantization types and image resolutions on RPi 5. Table 5. Performance of YOLOv11n with different quantization types and image resolutions on RPi 5. Table 6. Performance of YOLOv12n with different quantization types and image resolutions on RPi 5. 4.1.1. YOLOv5n YOLOv5n exhibited a reasonable compromise between detection accuracy and computational efficiency, as summarized in Table 3. At the native resolution of 640 × 640 pixels, the float32 model achieved an mAP@0.5 of 90.03 ± 0.95% at 3.5 FPS. Applying float16 and int8 quantization significantly increased inference speed to 8 FPS and 10 FPS, respectively, but reduced accuracy to 86.17 ± 7.27% and 85.93 ± 7.23%, respectively. The ONNX version preserved most of the detection performance, reaching an mAP@0.5 of 89.13 ± 1.42% at 6 FPS. When deployed on the EdgeTPU, YOLOv5n achieved 15 FPS with low CPU utilization (24%), although accuracy decreased to 85.93 ± 7.23%. Reducing the input resolution substantially improved inference speed: at 260 × 260 pixels, the EdgeTPU reached 50 FPS with an mAP@0.5 of 76.27 ± 8.23%, while at 192 × 192 pixels it achieved 90 FPS but with a severe mAP@0.5 drop to 53.93 ± 8.63%. These results indicate that YOLOv5n is suitable for low-latency applications but suffers from pronounced accuracy degradation under aggressive resolution reduction. 4.1.2. YOLOv8n YOLOv8n consistently outperformed YOLOv5n in both accuracy and resource efficiency, as shown in Table 4. At 640 × 640 pixels, the float32 model achieved an mAP@0.5 of 91.07 ± 0.61% at 2.7 FPS. The ONNX variant maintained high accuracy (89.80 ± 1.08%) while doubling inference speed to 6 FPS. Int8 quantization resulted in only a modest accuracy reduction (88.77 ± 3.31%) while increasing throughput to 4 FPS. The most significant gains were observed with EdgeTPU acceleration, where YOLOv8n achieved 30 FPS with CPU usage reduced to 12%, making it well-suited for real-time embedded deployment. Lower resolutions further improved performance while maintaining acceptable accuracy. At 260 × 260 pixels, the EdgeTPU reached 35 FPS with an mAP@0.5 of 79.43 ± 4.57%, and at 192 × 192 pixels it achieved 43 FPS with an mAP@0.5 of 61.10 ± 2.31%. Overall, YOLOv8n demonstrated a superior balance between precision, speed, and hardware utilization. 4.1.3. YOLOv11n As shown in Table 5, YOLOv11n achieved competitive detection accuracy but exhibited lower computational efficiency than YOLOv8n. At 640 × 640 pixels, the float32 model reached an mAP@0.5 of 90.33 ± 1.89% at 3 FPS, while the ONNX version increased throughput to 10 FPS with an mAP@0.5 of 88.27 ± 1.55%. Quantization to float16 and int8 improved inference speed to 8 FPS and 9 FPS, respectively, at the cost of moderate accuracy degradation. EdgeTPU execution was less effective for YOLOv11n, delivering only 7 FPS at full resolution. Although inference speed increased significantly at lower resolutions, reaching 53 FPS at 192 × 192 pixels on the EdgeTPU, this was accompanied by a substantial loss in detection accuracy, with the int8 model dropping to an mAP@0.5 of 54.07 ± 2.57%. These results suggest that YOLOv11n prioritizes accuracy but is less suitable for real-time embedded applications than YOLOv8n. 4.1.4. YOLOv12n YOLOv12n introduced incremental architectural refinements but did not yield a clear performance advantage for embedded inference, as summarized in Table 6. At 640 × 640 pixels, the float32 model achieved an mAP@0.5 of 91.03 ± 0.70% at 2 FPS, while the ONNX version reached 6 FPS with with an mAP@0.5 of 88.60 ± 1.57%. The int8-quantized model achieved 5.5 FPS with an mAP@0.5 of 78.13 ± 6.56%. EdgeTPU performance lagged behind that of YOLOv8n, achieving only 3.7 FPS at the highest resolution. Although inference speed improved at lower resolutions, reaching 50 FPS at 192 × 192 pixels with an mAP@0.5 of 54.30 ± 7.58%, the overall balance between accuracy and efficiency remained inferior to YOLOv8n. Consequently, YOLOv12n does not provide a compelling advantage for real-time embedded deployment. 4.1.5. Overall Comparison and Model Selection The comparative analysis of YOLOv5n, YOLOv8n, YOLOv11n, and YOLOv12n clearly identifies YOLOv8n as the most effective model for embedded object tracking applications. While YOLOv5n offers acceptable performance, it suffers from significant accuracy loss under aggressive quantization and resolution reduction. YOLOv11n and YOLOv12n introduce incremental improvements but fail to surpass YOLOv8n in terms of computational efficiency and EdgeTPU acceleration. YOLOv8n consistently maintained high detection accuracy under int8 quantization and achieved real-time inference at 30 FPS on the EdgeTPU with minimal CPU usage (12%). Its robustness across multiple resolutions and deployment formats makes it particularly well-suited for low-latency, energy-efficient embedded vision systems. During experiments using the Blender-generated traffic video containing five moving vehicles, YOLOv8n achieved an mAP@0.5 of 91.07 ± 0.61% at 640 × 640 pixels in float32 format and retained a strong mAP@0.5 of 88.77 ± 3.31% under int8 quantization. EdgeTPU execution further highlighted its efficiency, delivering high frame rates without compromising detection reliability. These characteristics confirm YOLOv8n as the most balanced and scalable solution for real-time object tracking on embedded platforms. Among the three runs, seed 42 yielded the strongest and most consistent validation performance for YOLOv8n at 640 × 640 across float32, ONNX, and int8/EdgeTPU deployments; therefore, it was selected as the representative checkpoint for embedded inference. Seed 0 performed best only under aggressive downscaling ( 192 × 192 ), whereas seed 42 dominated at the target deployment resolution. 4.2. YOLOv8n Assessment Based on the results presented in the previous tables and the trade-off analysis between detection accuracy and computational cost (high mAP@0.5 while remaining amenable to EdgeTPU acceleration and low CPU use), this subsection focuses exclusively on the YOLOv8n model. Among the evaluated configurations, YOLOv8n achieved the best overall balance, delivering high accuracy while maintaining low resource consumption. In addition, the model demonstrated strong robustness against false positives, an essential property for reliable object tracking in embedded systems. Given that the dataset is inherently imbalanced, metrics such as Precision, Recall, F1-score and the confusion matrix are particularly important for a reliable performance assessment. Figure 6 shows the confusion matrix for classes: bus, car, motorcycle, person, truck, van, and background. The confusion matrix was generated using the dataset shuffled and split with seed 42. Two clear points emerge from this matrix. First, distinct vehicle classes (bus, car, van, motorcycle) exhibit very low inter-class confusion, confirming that the model reliably differentiates among these categories in our dataset. Second, the person and truck classes show noticeably lower accuracy and are more often confused with background. This behavior suggests that background interference plays a significant role in missed detections, especially for these classes. As it will be discussed next, this pattern is consistent with the per-class average precision (AP) values and the visual examples in Figure 7. Figure 6. Confusion matrix. Color intensity represents normalized accuracy values from 0 to 1, ranging from white (lower values) to blue (higher values). Figure 7. Examples of detection failures caused by occlusion or overlap, highlighted with red boxes. The limitation of classes person and truck can be partially explained by challenging visual conditions in the dataset, where people and trucks often appear overlapped, partially occluded, or visually blended with the background, as illustrated in Figure 7. In such cases, inaccurate or ambiguous annotations may have led the model to associate foreground objects with background features, thereby increasing confusion in complex scenes. Despite this issue, the confusion matrix shows minimal misclassification across distinct foreground classes, demonstrating that YOLOv8n is effective at distinguishing among visually similar categories such as bus, car, van, and motorcycle. Further insight into the detection performance is provided by the Precision–Recall (PR) curves shown in Figure 8. The PR curve was generated using the dataset shuffled and split with seed 42. These curves illustrate the trade-off between Precision and Recall across different confidence thresholds and are a standard tool for evaluating object detection models. Figure 8. Precision and Recall curves for YOLOv8n. The mAP@0.5 complements this analysis by summarizing performance across all recall levels. YOLOv8n achieved a global mAP@0.5 of 95.6 with seed 42, indicating excellent overall detection capability. However, the person class obtained a notably medium lower score of 85.5 (with seed 42), which aligns with the confusion matrix observations. This performance gap highlights a potential limitation that should be carefully considered in real-world applications where accurate person detection is critical. Finally, the integration of the Coral accelerator proved to be a decisive factor for the target embedded application. The accelerator significantly reduced computational load on the RPi 5, enabling the deployment of a higher-resolution model (640 × 640 pixels) with with full-integer (int8) quantization (Edge TPU) weights without sacrificing detection accuracy. With hardware acceleration, the system achieved real-time frame-level inference at 30 FPS, a key practical requirement for edge-based object detection. Compared to CPU-only execution, inference speed increased approximately 6 times. Although alternative model configurations were evaluated, this setup offered the best overall performance. It was therefore selected for the final validation using the custom virtual dataset developed for this case study. 4.3. Distance and Speed Estimation on a Synthetic Dataset The quantitative evaluation of distance and speed estimation in this subsection is primarily conducted using a Blender-generated synthetic dataset, which provides precise control over vehicle speed, inter-object distance, and collision scenarios. This controlled environment enables rigorous assessment of metric accuracy and error bounds that are difficult to obtain from real-world data, given measurement uncertainty and annotation limitations. Consequently, the reported quantitative results establish internal validity and an upper bound on performance under idealized conditions. The experimental evaluation focuses on a representative urban road segment and camera setup selected to assess distance and speed estimation under controlled and realistic conditions. This scenario is not intended to cover the full diversity of smart-city environments, but rather to demonstrate the applicability of the proposed architecture to typical deployments. 4.3.1. Distance Calculation Assessment The distance-estimation algorithm was evaluated on two synthetic sequences from the Blender-generated dataset: (a) a rear-view five-vehicle sequence with fixed inter-vehicle spacings of 5, 11, and 24 m, Figure 9; and (b) a side-diagonal vehicle–pedestrian approach sequence designed to emulate a collision-risk situation, Figure 10. In both cases, distances were computed using the BC point of the detected bounding boxes, which may introduce a systematic offset when the BC does not coincide with the physical contact point (e.g., vehicle front bumper or pedestrian feet). Rear-View Multi-Vehicle Sequence (Fixed Spacings) This sequence contains 1500 frames. For each frame, the algorithm attempts to estimate three target distances (5 m, 11 m, and 24 m). However, due to bounding-box overlap and occasional occlusions, a valid estimate is not available for every target distance in every frame. Therefore, the statistics in Table 7 are computed only over the frames in which the corresponding distance could be measured. The Count column in the table reports exactly this: the number of valid frames used for each target distance (508 for 5 m, 806 for 11 m, and 439 for 24 m), totalling 1753 valid distance samples across the three targets. Figure 9. Five cars in uniform motion with fixed spacing over 5 m, 11 m, and 24 m segments. Figure 9. Five cars in uniform motion with fixed spacing over 5 m, 11 m, and 24 m segments.
Figure 10. Perspective of the simulated collision scenario involving a pedestrian and an approaching vehicle. Figure 10. Perspective of the simulated collision scenario involving a pedestrian and an approaching vehicle. Figure 11 compares the expected distances (red dashed) and the estimated distances (blue). As summarized in Table 7, the mean estimated distances were 4.85 m (target 5 m), 11.91 m (target 11 m), and 23.31 m (target 24 m). The corresponding average error distances were − 0.15 m, + 0.91 m, and − 0.69 m, respectively. To provide a single aggregate error indicator for this scenario, we compute the Average Error Distance (AED) across the three target distances A E D = | 4.85 − 5 | + | 11.91 − 11 | + | 23.31 − 24 | 3 = 0.58 m . (5) The largest instantaneous absolute error observed in this sequence was 5.20 m, occurring for the 24 m target when the minimum estimated distance reached 18.80 m ( Table 7). The algorithm outputs distances with a resolution of 0.01 m. Table 7. Statistical Metrics of Estimated Distances at Different Target Positions. Table 7.
